	 Specialists from PT Expert Security Center has been monitoring the activity of the Cobalt group since 2016. Today, the group is attacking financial institutions around the world. Over the past year, the Cobalt group has not only modified its main CobInt tools and COM-DLL dropper in conjunction with the more_eggs JavaScript backdoor but also used new delivery methods and new techniques to bypass protection at the initial stage of the attack. In this article, we would like to talk about new group tactics, delivery methods, and changes mainly in malware.

style="color:rgb(151, 161, 176)" class="Icon_icon__IyoOP Icon_md___TsqE"><svg role="img" focusable="false" aria-hidden="true" viewBox="0 0 20 20" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" fill="none"><path stroke="currentColor" stroke-linecap="round" d="m16 13-5.646-5.646a.5.5 0 0 0-.708 0L4 13"></path></svg></div></button></div><article><div class="flex flex-col gap-10 laptop:gap-15"><div class="GridLayout_grid-wrap__UGXQb GridLayout_grid-gap-medium__a2T51 ArticleContentContainer_container__0vDuu content-item"><div class="col-span-4 mobile:col-span-4 tablet:col-span-7 tablet-landscape:col-span-7 laptop:col-span-6 flex flex-col"><div class="HtmlBlock_article-content__4o6_U break-words HtmlBlock_deprecated__3I2_a"><article>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The PT Expert Security Center (PT ESC) has been monitoring the Cobalt group since <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/analytics/Cobalt-Snatch-eng.pdf" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/analytics/Cobalt-Snatch-eng.pdf">2016</a>. Currently the group targets financial organizations around the world. Two years ago, for example, their attacks caused over $14 million in damage. Over the last four years, we have <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/cobalt-strikes-back-an-evolving-multinational-threat-to-finance/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/cobalt-strikes-back-an-evolving-multinational-threat-to-finance/">released</a> several <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/new-bank-attacks/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/new-bank-attacks/">reports on attacks</a> linked to the group.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Over the last year, the group has not only modified its flagship tools CobInt and COM-DLL-Dropper in conjunction with the more_eggs JavaScript backdoor, but also started using new methods to deliver malware and bypass security in the initial stages of the kill chain. As a group whose activities have long been of interest to security researchers all over the world, the attackers are highly motivated to stay one step ahead.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/01_en.PNG" alt="Figure 1. Number of Cobalt attacks detected by PT ESC"/> <figcaption>Figure 1. Number of Cobalt attacks detected by PT ESC</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In 2019, the group conducted an average of three attacks per month. Although we do not know whether the attacks were successful, such frequency may indicate that the criminals possess substantial financial resources allowing them to maintain their infrastructure, update malware, and adopt new techniques.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The following histogram shows that in late 2019 the group started favoring CobInt over COM-DLL-Dropper.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/02_3_en.png" alt=" Figure 2. Number of attacks using COM-DLL-Dropper and CobInt in 2019"/> <figcaption> Figure 2. Number of attacks using COM-DLL-Dropper and CobInt in 2019</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The more_eggs JavaScript backdoor is detected by the ETPro ruleset, including in public sandboxes, whereas CobInt traffic does not trigger security mechanisms. In addition, CobInt downloads the main library from the command and control (C2) server directly to memory, while COM-DLL-Dropper saves to disk the obfuscated more_eggs, which is then executed in memory. Therefore, COM-DLL-Dropper leaves more artifacts on the infected machine.
</p>
<h2 class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">1. European Central Bank phishing website</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In late August 2019, we detected a CobInt attack that presumably targeted European financial institutions. We do not know whether the attack was successful. CobInt was dropped by a custom NSIS installer. We detected three versions of the dropper: for Chrome, Firefox, and Opera. Each dropper contained the same CobInt version and a browser-specific installer. Once launched, the dropper saved CobInt to the %TEMP% folder and then ran CobInt and the installer. Malware analysis proved that the droppers were distributed from the phishing website ecb-european[.]eu.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/03.png" alt="Figure 3. Phishing website main page"/> <figcaption>Figure 3. Phishing website main page</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The site was a copy of the European Central Bank website, except for a pop-up window that asked visitors to update the browser.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/04.png" alt="Figure 4. Pop-up window on the fake ECB website"/> <figcaption>Figure 4. Pop-up window on the fake ECB website</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Visitors who fell for the ruse downloaded the dropper to their computer. The page source code contained a link to the script that displayed the pop-up window.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/05.png" alt="Figure 5. Link to malicious script"/> <figcaption>Figure 5. Link to malicious script</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The configuration strings in the script contain links for four droppers (we could not obtain the first one) and allow creating links for Safari, Edge, and Internet Explorer. The strings also show the window start time after loading the page, how many times the window will be shown to a user, type of device on which the window will be displayed, and which banner will be shown to the user. In addition, the script detects bots, crawlers, and spiders.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/06.png" alt="Figure 6. Malicious script parameters"/> <figcaption>Figure 6. Malicious script parameters</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Here are alternative windows contained in the script:
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/07.png" alt="Figure 7. Alternative window specified in the script parameters"/> <figcaption>Figure 7. Alternative window specified in the script parameters</figcaption></figure> <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/08.png" alt="Figure 8. Alternative window specified in the script parameters"/> <figcaption>Figure 8. Alternative window specified in the script parameters</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 We do not know how the user landed on this website. Most likely, the user would be a victim of a phishing attack like many of those performed by Cobalt.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The framework in question is not unique. We believe that Cobalt purchased it on a darkweb forum. In an <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/research/netsupport-rat-installed-fake-update-notices" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.zscaler.com/blogs/research/netsupport-rat-installed-fake-update-notices">article</a> from November 2019, Zscaler described a similar scenario for spreading NetSupport RAT. The framework was placed on compromised sites, which showed visitors a corresponding pop-up window.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In yet another case, the malicious file Login_Details.img was also distributed from the site ecb-european[.]eu. Our colleagues from Group-IB have provided a <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.group-ib.com/blog/cobaltphishing" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.group-ib.com/blog/cobaltphishing">detailed analysis</a> of the malware.
</p>
<h2 class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2. Malicious VHD</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In late December 2019, we detected another CobInt loader used by Cobalt. The loader container was unusual. It was a virtual hard disk (VHD), presumably distributed by email.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The VHD format was originally developed by Connectix for their Virtual PC product. Microsoft acquired the product in 2003 and renamed it Microsoft Virtual PC. In 2005, the format became available to the public. Microsoft started using the VHD format in Hyper-V, the hypervisor-based virtualization technology. A VHD file may contain anything found on a physical hard drive, such as disk partitions and a file system with folders and files.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Windows 7 and newer systems include the ability to manually mount VHD files, such as via the MMC console. Starting with Windows 8, a user can mount a VHD by simply double-clicking the file. A mounted VHD disk image appears to Windows just like a normal hard disk.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In September 2019, the CERT/CC Blog published an article about the danger of VHD files and their possible use as an attack <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://insights.sei.cmu.edu/cert/2019/09/the-dangers-of-vhd-and-vhdx-files.html" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://insights.sei.cmu.edu/cert/2019/09/the-dangers-of-vhd-and-vhdx-files.html">vector</a>. The researcher Will Dorman showed that neither antivirus software nor the Mark of the Web alerts users about the potential harm of the contents of a VHD file downloaded from the Internet. Dorman created a malicious VHD container with EICAR inside and uploaded the result to VirusTotal. The malware was not detected by any antivirus engines. A VHD file is critical for operation of Hyper-V virtual machines. If this file is damaged or blocked, the virtual machine will not run. This may explain the rarity, or even absence, of antivirus detection. In documentation, Microsoft <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://support.microsoft.com/en-gb/help/3105657/recommended-antivirus-exclusions-for-hyper-v-hosts" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://support.microsoft.com/en-gb/help/3105657/recommended-antivirus-exclusions-for-hyper-v-hosts">recommends</a> excluding VHD files from antivirus scanning (as automatically is the case in Windows Defender). Otherwise, Hyper-V is susceptible to issues.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 It is possible that Cobalt used the findings of this research for their own purposes. Their VHD file was also not detected by any antivirus software when it first appeared on VirusTotal. Half a year later, the file was detected by just one antivirus engine, which is still very low.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/09.png" alt="Figure 9. Cobalt VHD detection level at the moment of attack"/> <figcaption>Figure 9. Cobalt VHD detection level at the moment of attack</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The VHD contains two CobInt files. One file has two invalid Google certificates appended to it in order to reduce the odds of detection.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/10_en.PNG" alt=" Figure 10. Certificates appended to a CobInt file"/> <figcaption> Figure 10. Certificates appended to a CobInt file</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Since VHD is in essence a container with a file system, one can search for artifacts inside VHD files. For example, we found an image with text of a fake HSBC antifraud message in the unallocated space of a VHD file.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/11.png" alt="Figure 11. Image in unallocated space of a VHD file"/> <figcaption>Figure 11. Image in unallocated space of a VHD file</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The attackers may have inadvertently left the artifact when reallocating space in the container: the same image was used as the CobInt icon and stored in the group&#x27;s resources.
</p>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.1. CobInt analysis</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Once the VHD is mounted, a user must manually run one of the files. The two files are identical in terms of functions. When run, either of the CobInt files downloads the main library from the C2 server as an HTML file.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 There are a few changes in comparison to the algorithm <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.proofpoint.com/us/threat-insight/post/new-modular-downloaders-fingerprint-systems-part-3-cobint" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.proofpoint.com/us/threat-insight/post/new-modular-downloaders-fingerprint-systems-part-3-cobint">described by ProofPoint in 2018</a>:
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/12.png" alt="Figure 12. Example of obfuscation of the main library"/> <figcaption>Figure 12. Example of obfuscation of the main library</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 First, all tags are removed and their contents are ignored.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/13.png" alt="Figure 13. Tag removal"/> <figcaption>Figure 13. Tag removal</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Next, periods, commas, and spaces are processed. All characters after these symbols are uppercased (the value 0x20 is subtracted).
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/14.png" alt="Figure 14. Removing unnecessary characters and switching letters to uppercase"/> <figcaption>Figure 14. Removing unnecessary characters and switching letters to uppercase</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Next, data is decoded from Base64 and decrypted by XOR with a 4-byte key that is initialized with the preceding value of the decrypted data at each iteration. At each iteration, the current round&#x27;s 4 bytes are subtracted from those of the previous round, after which the key is the 4-byte value of the input buffer of the previous round.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/15.png" alt="Figure 15. First XOR level"/> <figcaption>Figure 15. First XOR level</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Once decryption finishes, the second-stage decryptor takes over. In essence, it consists of an XOR decryption cycle using a 4-byte key that is the same for the entire stage. The output of this stage will be a .dll library, which is the payload.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Data decoded from Base64 is shown in Figure 16. A 4-byte preset for the first decryptor is highlighted in red and will remain the same during the second stage. The rest of the data is highlighted in yellow.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/16.png" alt="Figure 16. Data decoded from Base64"/> <figcaption>Figure 16. Data decoded from Base64</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The picture changes after decryption (Figure 17). The encryption key is clearly visible due to a long series of zeros in the executable file that, after encryption, contain the keystream in pure form.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/17.png" alt="Figure 17. Data after removal of the first XOR level"/> <figcaption>Figure 17. Data after removal of the first XOR level</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The second decryption gives us a valid PE file (Figure 18). We could not figure out the purpose of the first eight bytes: they are not used anywhere in the loader.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/18.png" alt="Figure 18. Deobfuscated library"/> <figcaption>Figure 18. Deobfuscated library</figcaption></figure>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.2. Main library analysis</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Once an event is created and the necessary parameters are initialized, the domain is decrypted. Then the function for generating the remaining part of the address is called.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/19.png" alt="Figure 19. Algorithm for generating remaining part of the address"/> <figcaption>Figure 19. Algorithm for generating remaining part of the address</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 After the full C2 server address is generated, the library decrypts the necessary parameters to create HTTP fields, adds them to a request, and sends the request to the server. The server response contains plugins that the library loads into its address space using ReflectiveLoader.
</p>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.3. Decryption of plugins</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Like the main library, the plugins are sent by the server as HTML pages. The first stage of input transformation is similar to what happens during the library download. The difference is that all periods, commas, and spaces are ignored, and all characters are lowercased.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 After the initial transformation, the obtained data is decoded from a–z to 0x00—0xff. For this, a previously unseen decoding procedure is used. It is based on transforming input values depending on the current value, previous value (in some cases), and values of the global counter.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/20.png" alt="Figure 20. Plugin decoding algorithm"/> <figcaption>Figure 20. Plugin decoding algorithm</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The decoding is followed by two decryption cycles.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/21.png" alt="Figure 21. First decryption cycle"/> <figcaption>Figure 21. First decryption cycle</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The first decryption key is in the application code, hard-coded at an offset that takes only two values.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 To carry out the second decryption cycle, the last byte of data is read. This byte is the length of the encryption key for the second cycle. The file is read at this number of bytes (plus one) from the end. After the key is read, the data is decrypted, except for the key itself. In Figure 22, the key length is highlighted in red and the key itself is highlighted in yellow.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/22.png" alt="Figure 22. XOR key example"/> <figcaption>Figure 22. XOR key example</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The last stage is decryption with a 4-byte key, which is also easily obtained by analyzing the series of zeros in the PE header.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/23.png" alt="Figure 23. XOR key in encrypted data"/> <figcaption>Figure 23. XOR key in encrypted data</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Our analysis detected two types of downloaded plugins: one that steals the names of running processes plus a screen capture module. Both plugins use standard WinAPIs to obtain data, as well as the same function as the main library in the export for reflective process loading.
</p>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">2.4. Traffic decryption</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The library sends the data collected by the plugins to the server.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Here is an example of traffic:
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/24_1.png" alt="Figure 24. Encoded data collected by plugin"/> <figcaption>Figure 24. Encoded data collected by plugin</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 First, the traffic is encrypted by a randomly generated key of arbitrary length. The key is inserted into the packet with indication of its length.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Next, the data is encrypted with a hard-coded 64-byte key, the same one that was used to decrypt the library. After that, the same encoding algorithm is applied. An equals sign (=) and the generated sequence of 8 to 16 a–z characters are added to the beginning.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Example of decoded and decrypted traffic from the previous packet:
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/25_1.png" alt="Figure 25. Decoded data"/> <figcaption>Figure 25. Decoded data</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Packet creation and transformation function:
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/26.png" alt="Figure 26. Algorithm for creating packet with data"/> <figcaption>Figure 26. Algorithm for creating packet with data</figcaption></figure>
<h2 class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">3. BIFF macro</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In March 2020, we detected an XLS document from Cobalt that downloaded and ran the COM-DLL-Dropper. The document contained the rather old Excel 4.0 macro format and was almost invisible to antivirus software (1 positive verdict out of 60 on VirusTotal).
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/27.png" alt="Figure 27. Number of antivirus verdicts on VirusTotal during first upload of the file with Excel 4.0 macro"/> <figcaption>Figure 27. Number of antivirus verdicts on VirusTotal during first upload of the file with Excel 4.0 macro</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 This macro standard is 20 years old. The standard is peculiar in that the macro is stored in worksheet cells (not stored in a VBA project), and the worksheet itself can be hidden in Excel. The macro therefore will not be in a VBA stream, but in a BIFF (Binary Interchange File Format) record.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 If we open the document in Excel, we see one worksheet and no VBA project macros. However, Excel all the same detects the macro and blocks it from running.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The olevba3.py utility from oletools can be used to detect this macro.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/28.png" alt="Figure 28. Result of olevba3.py executiona"/> <figcaption>Figure 28. Result of olevba3.py execution</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
</p>
 By running the utility, we see that one of the document worksheets has the status &quot;very hidden&quot; and is of the Excel 4.0 macro type. Because of this status, the worksheet will be invisible in the Excel interface and, what&#x27;s more, it cannot be made visible from the interface either. It can only be made visible by Visual Basic or by manually modifying the document&#x27;s bytes.
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
 <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.aldeid.com/wiki/BiffView" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.aldeid.com/wiki/BiffView">The BiffView utility</a> provides a more workable view of the BIFF structure. After parsing the initial document, we see that a page named sygfdfdfdesie has the attribute &quot;very hidden.&quot; We change this parameter to 1 or 0 in a hex editor.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/29_en.PNG" alt="Figure 29. Structure of malicious document worksheets"/> <figcaption>Figure 29. Structure of malicious document worksheets</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 When the initial document is opened in the Name Manager, one of the formulas runs automatically:
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/30_en.PNG" alt="Figure 30. Macro formula that runs when the document is opened"/> <figcaption>Figure 30. Macro formula that runs when the document is opened</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The initial formula launches a long chain of commands, such as CONCATENATE, RUN, CHAR, and CALL, which will lead to the loading and launch of COM-DLL-Dropper. The commands are scattered across the Excel cells, complicating analysis.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/26.png" alt="Figure 31. Macro formulas leading to loading and launch of COM-DLL-Dropper"/> <figcaption>Figure 31. Macro formulas leading to loading and launch of COM-DLL-Dropper</figcaption></figure>
<h2 class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4. COM-DLL-Dropper analysis</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 In early April 2020, we detected a new version of COM-DLL-Dropper. Its functions are different from everything we had seen before. However, the more_eggs JavaScript backdoor payload remained the same.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Cobalt first started using COM-DLL-Dropper in the summer of 2017 and is still using it to deliver more_eggs, which is contained in the dropper in encrypted and archived form.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 A few facts about the dropper:
</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>It is written completely in PureBasic.</li>
	<li>It uses numerous anti-analysis techniques.</li>
	<li>It contains an encrypted and archived JavaScript loader, JavaScript backdoor, and a legitimate utility for modifying the command line to launch more_eggs.</li>
	<li>It has a built-in obfuscator for the hard-coded JavaScript backdoor and JavaScript loader</li>
</ul>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4.1. PE file external structure</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 All the studied items are PE-DLL files to be registered by regsvr32. In addition to exports called by regsvr32, each sample has different sets of exports typical of legitimate DLL files. Cobalt attempted to mask COM-DLL-Dropper by using third-party exports. Figure 32 shows the most popular exports used in the malware files (total of 249 unique exports).
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/32.png" alt="Figure 32. Most popular COM-DLL dropper exports"/> <figcaption>Figure 32. Most popular COM-DLL dropper exports</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 These exports contain stubs that generally do not actually do anything. Judging by the names of the exports, the droppers were masked to resemble media application libraries. In 2019, the malware was updated with the DllInstall export, which is also called by regsvr32, and the main dropper code was moved to the export.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Before describing the malware code, we should touch on the PureBasic code. The information we provide here is the result of analyzing malware samples. We did not study the compiler itself and therefore are forced to make certain assumptions. However, the described entities helped us in our analysis, which is why we are sharing them here. All the names in screenshots were made up for the purposes of interpreting the malware code.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Our analysis requires two entities: strings and object arrays. PureBasic strings are stored in a special buffer. They are allocated and released without using a system API. Figure 33 shows the process of string allocation. During program initialization, a separate heap is created for strings by calling HeapCreate().
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/33.png" alt="Figure 33. String workings"/> <figcaption>Figure 33. String workings</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 A common pattern for working with this entity is as follows:
</p>
<ol class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ol__ZnTfe weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>Allocate a string to storage from a constant.</li>
	<li>Operate on the string.</li>
	<li>Update the global string which is usually allocated on the heap. After the update is completed, move back the node index. This operation is somewhat similar to pop().</li>
</ol>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The string storage structures do not allow storing the size of the added string. Instead, before starting any operation with the string, the program saves the previous index of the node and then passes it to the update operation. The difference between the indices is the string size.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 We will not describe object arrays here in detail; suffice it to say that a special header before each array stores information about the size, type, and number of elements. The header occupies 18h bytes. Therefore, the space allocated for the array of objects can be calculated as <em>size of element × number of elements + 18h</em>.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 To get a clearer picture, refer to this description of functions that are presented in the screenshots a bit later.
</p>

<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4.2. Anti-analysis</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 To find the needed API functions, non-standard hash sums obtained from the functions&#x27; names are used. Each hash sum is obtained by taking the CRC32 value and then performing XOR with a constant. The samples have different constants. This is why Table 3 also includes CRC32 values without the constant-value XOR.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The new version of COM-DLL-Dropper has strings encrypted with the RC4 algorithm, whereas the older version used XOR.
</p>

<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Key bruteforcing for string decryption is not &quot;complete.&quot; In fact, most of the key consists of a hard-coded prefix found in the code. The end of the key is a decimal number. Therefore, <em>key = prefix + number.</em>
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/34.png" alt="Figure 34. RC4 key bruteforce"/> <figcaption>Figure 34. RC4 key bruteforce</figcaption></figure>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4.3. JavaScript generators</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The dropper creates two files. The first is a JavaScript loader, and the second is a scriptlet containing the encrypted more_eggs backdoor. Both scripts are generated.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The generation template is saved among malware samples. The inserted data varies. The template contains tokens and JavaScript parts that are concatenated in series. Figure 35 shows part of generation of the JavaScript loader and examples of the used JavaScript parts.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/35.png" alt="Figure 35. Generation of a part of the loader"/> <figcaption>Figure 35. Generation of a part of the loader</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Several obfuscation templates are built into the generator:
</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>Compensatory disguising of constants</li>
	<li>Generation of random variable names</li>
	<li>Insertion of encrypted strings</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Each generator contains a pool of names that are generated prior to starting creation of the script. These names are then used in JavaScript. Figure 35 shows a local variable named lpbobj_arraywcs__JSObjectPool. Figure 36 shows the pool initialization cycle.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/36.png" alt="Figure 36. Example of filling the pool of names used in the script"/> <figcaption>Figure 36. Example of filling the pool of names used in the script&gt;</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Each name available to be used in the script contains two parts: a random prefix (which is created once for the entire script) and a random decimal number (limited to a set number of characters). Figure 37 shows the name generation scheme and the result obtained in the script.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/37.png" alt="Figure 37. Generation of names available to be used in the script"/> <figcaption>Figure 37. Generation of names available to be used in the script</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Numeric constants are obfuscated with a function that applies a random arithmetic operation from a set hard-coded in the program and then inserts the opposite operation in the script. Thus, the inserted expression balances out the obfuscated constant. The second arithmetic operation argument is also generated randomly from a hard-coded range of values.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/38.png" alt="Figure 38. Generation of obfuscated constants"/> <figcaption>Figure 38. Generation of obfuscated constants</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The payload is encoded using RC4 and Base91 and inserted in the script. The implementations of RC4 and the Base91 decoder are also inserted in the scripts.
</p>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4.4. Persistence</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Depending on its rights in the system, the dropper entrenches itself on the infected machine using the following methods:
</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>By using Task Scheduler</li>
	<li>By using the registry key Environment\UserInitMprLogonScript</li>
	<li>By using the registry key Software\Microsoft\Windows\CurrentVersion\Run</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 For all three methods, the value written by the dropper is the same, containing the command for launching the JavaScript loader.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/39.png" alt="Figure 39. Example of persistence via UserInitMprLogonScript"/> <figcaption>Figure 39. Example of persistence via UserInitMprLogonScript</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 To configure a task created by the dropper, a special XML file is generated. Part of it is stored in the dropper in encrypted form, and another part is generated while running.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/40.png" alt="Figure 40. Decrypted part of XML"/> <figcaption>Figure 40. Decrypted part of XML</figcaption></figure> <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/41.png" alt="Figure 41. Creating end for the XML file"/> <figcaption>Figure 41. Creating end for the XML file</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The resulting XML file is saved with a random name consisting of hexadecimal characters. Subsequently, this XML file is passed to schtasks.exe as the /XML parameter value.
</p>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4.5. Running the payload</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 COM-DLL-Dropper saves three files to disk:
</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>Obfuscated JavaScript loader</li>
	<li>Obfuscated JavaScript backdoor</li>
	<li>Legitimate utility for modifying the command line in order to launch the more_eggs JavaScript backdoor</li>
</ul>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The main backdoor is launched with the help of a known <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://pentestlab.blog/2017/07/06/applocker-bypass-msxsl/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://pentestlab.blog/2017/07/06/applocker-bypass-msxsl/">AppLocker bypass technique</a> using the msxsl utility. The commands look as follows:
</p>
<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>“C:\Users\\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\msxsl.exe”</li>
	<li>“C:\Users\\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\[javascript_downloader_name].txt”</li>
	<li>“C:\Users\\AppData\Roaming\Microsoft\[javascript_backdoor_name].txt”</li>
</ul>
<h3 class="break-words size_s__3_Wdt color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">4.6. JavaScript backdoor functionality</h3>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The JavaScript backdoor saved to disk by the new COM-DLL-Dropper has version 6.6.
</p>
 <figure> <img src="https://pt-corp.storage.yandexcloud.net/upload/corporate/ww-en/images/esc/cobalt-2020/42.png" alt="Figure 42. Backdoor header"/> <figcaption>Figure 42. Backdoor header</figcaption></figure>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 This backdoor has been used by Cobalt since 2017. It is executed in memory and always has a low number of antivirus verdicts.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 The main capabilities of the backdoor are as follows:
</p>
<ol class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ol__ZnTfe weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
	<li>Traffic encryption with RC4 and Base91</li>
	<li>Execution of operator commands (in this version, the more_eggs command that gave the backdoor its name was absent):</li>
	<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
		<li>exec: download and run file (.exe or .dll)</li>
		<li>gtfo: uninstall</li>
		<li>more_onion: run script</li>
		<li>via_c: execute command using &quot;cmd.exe /C&quot;</li>
		<li>more_time: execute command using &quot;cmd.exe /C&quot;, with the result being saved to a temporary file. After that, the file is read and deleted, and its contents are encoded with Base64 and sent to the server.</li>
	</ul>
	<li>Check of the process list for antivirus protection and researcher software by comparing CRC32 values (derived from the name of each process, without extension and in lower case) against hard-coded values.</li>
	<li>Reconnaissance:</li>
	<ul class="listStyles_list__w6p69 listStyles_ul__CFFG0 weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA margin_xs__lb5TS">
		<li>Date of system installation</li>
		<li>Infected machine&#x27;s IP address</li>
		<li>System type (server or desktop)</li>
		<li>Windows version (from XP to 10)</li>
	</ul>
</ol>
<h2 class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">Conclusion</h2>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
	 Cobalt keeps attacking financial organizations around the world, refining its TTPs, and inventing ever-more sophisticated ways to bypass defenses. Due to quarantine-related measures, many employees of financial companies are now working remotely, outside the protection offered by corporate security solutions. Moreover, many threat actors are using COVID-19 as a lure in their attacks, as <a target="_blank" rel="nofollow noreferrer" href="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/covid-19-and-new-year-greetings-the-higaisa-group/" class="linkStyles_links-gray__auxKT weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_links-gray__IEMoh linkStyles_underlined__4E3jf" title="https://www.ptsecurity.com/ww-en/analytics/pt-esc-threat-intelligence/covid-19-and-new-year-greetings-the-higaisa-group/">the Higaisa group</a> has done. It is possible that Cobalt, too, will try to weaponize such concern.
</p>
<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
 <b>Authors</b>: Denis Kuvshinov, Sergey Tarasov, Daniil Koloskov, PT ESC
</p>

<h2 class="break-words size_m__P_9U_ color_primary__z_39p margin_none__1sjaB">MITRE TTPs</h2>
<div class="table-container">
	<table class="table-article">
	<thead>
	<tr>
		<th>
			 Tactic
		</th>
		<th>
			 ID
		</th>
		<th>
			 Name
		</th>
	</tr>
	</thead>
	<tbody>
	<tr>
		<td>
			<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
				 Initial Access
			</p>
		</td>
		<td>
			<p class="weight_regular__5MYqU size_l__TtckT margin_none__1sjaB color_secondary-plus__47VrA">
				 T1193
